Apart from these, [Tokyo Tower](/wiki/Tokyo_Tower "Tokyo Tower") (333m) and [Tokyo Sky Tree](/wiki/Tokyo_Skytree "Tokyo Skytree") (634m) feature high-elevation observation decks; the latter is the tallest tower in both Japan and the world, and the second tallest structure in the world after the [Burj Khalifa](/wiki/Burj_Khalifa "Burj Khalifa") in [Dubai](/wiki/Dubai "Dubai").^[\[13\]](#cite_note-skytree-15)^ With a scheduled completion date in 2027, [Torch Tower](/wiki/Torch_Tower_(Japan) "Torch Tower (Japan)") (385m) will overtake [Azabudai Hills Mori JP Tower](/wiki/Azabudai_Hills "Azabudai Hills") (325.2m) as the tallest building in Tokyo.{#cite_ref-skytree_15-1}

[Kenzo Tange](/wiki/Kenz%C5%8D_Tange "Kenzō Tange") designed notable contemporary buildings in Tokyo, including [Yoyogi National Gymnasium](/wiki/Yoyogi_National_Gymnasium "Yoyogi National Gymnasium") (1964), [St. Mary's Cathedral](/wiki/St._Mary%27s_Cathedral,_Tokyo "St. Mary's Cathedral, Tokyo") (1967), and the [Tokyo Metropolitan Government Building](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government_Building "Tokyo Metropolitan Government Building") (1991). [Kisho Kurokawa](/wiki/Kisho_Kurokawa "Kisho Kurokawa") was also active in the city, and his works there include the [National Art Center](/wiki/The_National_Art_Center,_Tokyo "The National Art Center, Tokyo") (2005) and the [Nakagin Capsule Tower](/wiki/Nakagin_Capsule_Tower "Nakagin Capsule Tower") (1972). Other notable contemporary buildings in Tokyo include the [Tokyo Dome](/wiki/Tokyo_Dome "Tokyo Dome"), [Mode Gakuen Cocoon Tower](/wiki/Mode_Gakuen_Cocoon_Tower "Mode Gakuen Cocoon Tower"), [Roppongi Hills](/wiki/Roppongi_Hills "Roppongi Hills"), [Tokyo International Forum](/wiki/Tokyo_International_Forum "Tokyo International Forum"), and [Asahi Beer Hall](/wiki/Asahi_Beer_Hall "Asahi Beer Hall").  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree_%28cropped%29.JPG/1500px-Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree_%28cropped%29.JPG)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree_(cropped).JPG "A panoramic view of Tokyo from the Tokyo Skytree")  
[](/wiki/File:Tokyo_from_the_top_of_the_SkyTree_(cropped).JPG "File:Tokyo from the top of the SkyTree (cropped).JPG")
A panoramic view of Tokyo from the [Tokyo Skytree](/wiki/Tokyo_Skytree "Tokyo Skytree")  

Demographics {#Demographics}
----------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Demographics")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Tokyo_prefecture_population_pyramid_in_2020.svg/313px-Tokyo_prefecture_population_pyramid_in_2020.svg.png)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_prefecture_population_pyramid_in_2020.svg) Tokyo metropolis population pyramid in 2020

| Year |            |        |
| 1920 | 3,699,428  |  ---   |
| 1925 | 4,485,144  | +21.2% |
| 1930 | 5,408,678  | +20.6% |
| 1935 | 6,369,919  | +17.8% |
| 1940 | 7,354,971  | +15.5% |
| 1945 | 3,488,284  | −52.6% |
| 1950 | 6,277,500  | +80.0% |
| 1955 | 8,037,084  | +28.0% |
| 1960 | 9,683,802  | +20.5% |
| 1965 | 10,869,244 | +12.2% |
| 1970 | 11,408,071 | +5.0%  |
| 1975 | 11,673,554 | +2.3%  |
| 1980 | 11,618,281 | −0.5%  |
| 1985 | 11,829,363 | +1.8%  |
| 1990 | 11,855,563 | +0.2%  |
| 1995 | 11,773,605 | −0.7%  |
| 2000 | 12,064,101 | +2.5%  |
| 2005 | 12,576,601 | +4.2%  |
| 2010 | 13,159,388 | +4.6%  |
| 2015 | 13,515,271 | +2.7%  |
| 2020 | 13,982,112 | +3.5%  |
|------|------------|--------|
[Historical population]

As of October 2012, the official [intercensal estimate](/wiki/Intercensal_estimate "Intercensal estimate") showed 13.506 million people in Tokyo, with 9.214 million living within Tokyo's 23 wards.^[\[141\]](#cite_note-population2015-143)^ During the daytime, the population swells by over 2.5 million as workers and students commute from adjacent areas. This effect is even more pronounced in the three central wards of [Chiyoda](/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo "Chiyoda, Tokyo"), [Chūō](/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D,_Tokyo "Chūō, Tokyo"), and [Minato](/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo "Minato, Tokyo"), whose collective population as of the 2005 National Census was 326,000 at night, but 2.4 million during the day.^[\[142\]](#cite_note-population-144)^{#cite_ref-population_144-0}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Tokyo_historical_population.gif/313px-Tokyo_historical_population.gif)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_historical_population.gif) Tokyo historical population since 1920

According to April 2024 official estimates, [Setagaya](/wiki/Setagaya "Setagaya") (942,003), [Nerima](/wiki/Nerima "Nerima") (752,608), and [Ota](/wiki/%C5%8Cta,_Tokyo "Ōta, Tokyo") (748,081) were the most populous wards and municipalities in Tokyo. The least inhabited of all Tokyo municipalities are remote island villages such as [Aogashima](/wiki/Aogashima,_Tokyo "Aogashima, Tokyo") (150), [Mikurajima](/wiki/Mikurajima,_Tokyo "Mikurajima, Tokyo") (289), and [Toshima](/wiki/To-shima,_Tokyo "To-shima, Tokyo") (306).^[\[143\]](#cite_note-145)^{#cite_ref-145}  

### Age structure and average age {#Age_structure_and_average_age}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=25 "Edit section: Age structure and average age")\]

In 2021, Tokyo's average and median ages were both 45.5 years old. This is below the national median age of 49.0, placing Tokyo among the youngest regions in Japan. 16.8% of the population was below 15, while 34.6% was above 65.^[\[144\]](#cite_note-146)^ In the same year, the youngest municipalities in Tokyo were [Mikura-jima](/wiki/Mikura-jima "Mikura-jima") (average age 40.72), Chuo (41.92), and Chiyoda (42.07), while the oldest included [Okutama](/wiki/Okutama,_Tokyo "Okutama, Tokyo") (59.11) and [Miyake](/wiki/Miyake,_Tokyo "Miyake, Tokyo") (53.82).^[\[145\]](#cite_note-147)^{#cite_ref-147}  

### Immigration {#Immigration}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=26 "Edit section: Immigration")\]

In 1889, the [Home Ministry](/wiki/Home_Ministry "Home Ministry") recorded 1,375,937 people in [Tokyo City](/wiki/Tokyo_City "Tokyo City") and a total of 1,694,292 people in [Tokyo-fu](/wiki/Tokyo_Prefecture_(1868%E2%80%931943) "Tokyo Prefecture (1868–1943)").^[\[146\]](#cite_note-148)^ In the same year, a total of 779 foreign nationals were recorded as residing in Tokyo. The most common nationality was English (209 residents), followed by American (182) and Chinese nationals (137).^[\[147\]](#cite_note-149)^{#cite_ref-149}

As of January 2024, Tokyo had 647,416 foreign nationals registered as residents, with China, South Korea, Vietnam, the Philippines, Nepal, Taiwan, and the United States each having more than 20,000 nationals living there as citizens.^[\[148\]](#cite_note-pop-detail-150)^ Since the COVID-19 pandemic ended, Tokyo's foreign population has increased significantly, now nearly 20% above the January 2022 population of 546,436.^[\[149\]](#cite_note-151)^ There is no official survey of race or place of birth as of June 2024.{#cite_ref-151}  

### Dialects {#Dialects}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=27 "Edit section: Dialects")\]  
See also: [Tokyo dialect](/wiki/Tokyo_dialect "Tokyo dialect")

[Japanese](/wiki/Japanese_language "Japanese language") is the primary language spoken throughout the metropolis, though regional and socio-economic differences can be heard. Traditionally, dialects in Tokyo are classified into two groups: the Yamanote dialect and the Shitamachi dialect. The former has traditionally been spoken in the upper- and upper-middle-class residential area of Yamanote, which includes [Bancho](/wiki/Banch%C5%8D "Banchō"), [Kojimachi](/wiki/K%C5%8Djimachi "Kōjimachi"), [Koishikawa](/wiki/Koishikawa "Koishikawa"), [Kudan](/wiki/Kudankita "Kudankita"), [Yotsuya](/wiki/Yotsuya "Yotsuya"), [Azabu](/wiki/Azabu "Azabu"), and [Akasaka](/wiki/Akasaka,_Tokyo "Akasaka, Tokyo"). During the [Edo period](/wiki/Edo_period "Edo period"), these neighborhoods were occupied by *[Daimyo](/wiki/Daimyo "Daimyo")* and other powerful [samurai](/wiki/Samurai "Samurai") families and the dialect evolved largely based on their way of speech. Standard Japanese pronunciation is largely based on this accent and spread across the country with the introduction of [radio](/wiki/Radio "Radio"). The Shitamachi dialect, in contrast, has been associated with the *[Chōnin](/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dnin "Chōnin")* district of Shitamachi and retains many characteristics of the accents spoken there in the Edo era.^[\[150\]](#cite_note-152)^ However, socio-economic changes in the post-war period and the large influx of people moving from other areas have largely blurred these distinctions in recent years. It has been reported that young generations are not as aware of the differences in dialects as their parents' and grandparents' generations were.^[\[151\]](#cite_note-153)^{#cite_ref-153}

The [Hachijō dialect](/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D_language "Hachijō language"), spoken primarily in [Hachijōjima](/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima "Hachijō-jima") and [Aogashima](/wiki/Aogashima "Aogashima"), descended from 6th-8th century [Eastern Old Japanese](/wiki/Eastern_Old_Japanese "Eastern Old Japanese") and has fewer than 1,000 speakers.^[\[152\]](#cite_note-154)^^[\[153\]](#cite_note-155)^ [Bonin English](/wiki/Bonin_English "Bonin English") is a creole spoken in the [Ogasawara Islands](/wiki/Bonin_Islands "Bonin Islands"), derived from English and Japanese,^[\[154\]](#cite_note-Long2006-156)^ as the islands' population historically consisted of people of Japanese, British, American, Hawaiian, and Polynesian origins, mostly mixed-race.^[\[155\]](#cite_note-:12-157)^{#cite_ref-:12_157-0}  

Economy {#Economy}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=28 "Edit section: Economy")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Tokyo_Marunouchi_in_autumn.jpg/300px-Tokyo_Marunouchi_in_autumn.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_Marunouchi_in_autumn.jpg) [Marunouchi](/wiki/Marunouchi "Marunouchi"), the main business district [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Sector_Breakdown_of_2021_GDP_of_Tokyo_Metropolis.png/300px-Sector_Breakdown_of_2021_GDP_of_Tokyo_Metropolis.png)](/wiki/File:Sector_Breakdown_of_2021_GDP_of_Tokyo_Metropolis.png) Sector breakdown of 2021 GDP of Tokyo

Tokyo's gross regional product was 113.7 trillion yen or US$1.04 trillion in FY2021 and accounted for 20.7% of the country's total economic output, which converts to 8.07 million yen or US$73,820 per capita.^[\[15\]](#cite_note-:3-17)^ By sector, Wholesale and Retail was the largest contributor, accounting for 21.5% of the total output. This was followed by Real Estate (13.5%), Professional, Scientific and Technical (12.2%), Information and Communications (11.7%), Finance and Insurance (7.6%), Manufacturing (7.0%), and Healthcare (6.7%). Agriculture, Forestry and Fishery, and Mining combined accounted for less than 0.1% of the economic output.^[\[156\]](#cite_note-158)^ As these numbers suggest, Tokyo's economy is heavily dependent on the tertiary sector. As the [Greater Tokyo Area](/wiki/Greater_Tokyo_Area "Greater Tokyo Area"), it has the [second-largest metropolitan economy in the world](/wiki/List_of_cities_by_GDP "List of cities by GDP"), after [Greater New York](/wiki/New_York_metropolitan_area "New York metropolitan area"), with a gross metropolitan product estimated at US$2 trillion. The area's economy is slightly smaller than [Canada's economy](/wiki/Economy_of_Canada "Economy of Canada") while being slightly larger than [Mexico's](/wiki/Economy_of_Mexico "Economy of Mexico"), according to IMF estimates from the same year.^[\[157\]](#cite_note-159)^{#cite_ref-159}

Tokyo's business districts are concentrated in four central wards: [Chiyoda](/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo "Chiyoda, Tokyo") ([Marunouchi](/wiki/Marunouchi "Marunouchi"), [Otemachi](/wiki/%C5%8Ctemachi "Ōtemachi"), [Kasumigaseki](/wiki/Kasumigaseki "Kasumigaseki")), [Chuo](/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D,_Tokyo "Chūō, Tokyo") ([Nihombashi](/wiki/Nihonbashi "Nihonbashi"), [Kyobashi](/wiki/Ky%C5%8Dbashi,_Tokyo "Kyōbashi, Tokyo"), [Yaesu](/wiki/Yaesu "Yaesu")), [Minato](/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo "Minato, Tokyo") ([Shimbashi](/wiki/Shinbashi "Shinbashi"), [Shiodome](/wiki/Shiodome "Shiodome"), [Toranomon](/wiki/Toranomon "Toranomon")), and [Shinjuku](/wiki/Shinjuku "Shinjuku") ([West Shinjuku](/wiki/Nishi-Shinjuku "Nishi-Shinjuku")). The 23 Special Wards of Tokyo had 73.5 million m^2^ of office space as of January 2022.^[\[158\]](#cite_note-160)^{#cite_ref-160}

In 2023, 29 of the *[Fortune Global 500](/wiki/Fortune_Global_500 "Fortune Global 500")* companies were headquartered in Tokyo, which was the second highest concentration in the world after [Beijing](/wiki/Beijing "Beijing").^[\[159\]](#cite_note-161)^ Notably, around 20 of them are based in Marunouchi, such as [MUFG](/wiki/Mitsubishi_UFJ_Financial_Group "Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group"), [Mitsubish Corp.](/wiki/Mitsubishi_Corporation "Mitsubishi Corporation") and [Hitachi](/wiki/Hitachi "Hitachi").^[\[160\]](#cite_note-162)^ Tokyo was rated by the [Economist Intelligence Unit](/wiki/Economist_Intelligence_Unit "Economist Intelligence Unit") as the most expensive (highest [cost-of-living](/wiki/Cost-of-living_index "Cost-of-living index")) city in the world for 14 years in a row ending in 2006, when it was replaced by [Oslo](/wiki/Oslo "Oslo"), and later [Paris](/wiki/Paris "Paris").^[\[161\]](#cite_note-163)^^[\[162\]](#cite_note-164)^ However, years of deflation and an extremely weak yen starting in 2022 due to Japan's low interest rates made the cost of living in Tokyo 31% cheaper than in [New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City") in 2023, which is roughly the same as in [Beijing](/wiki/Beijing "Beijing") and [Manchester](/wiki/Manchester "Manchester") according to the 2023 EIU rankings.^[\[163\]](#cite_note-165)^ [Henley \& Partners](/wiki/Henley_%26_Partners "Henley & Partners") estimated that there were 298,300 individuals with a net worth of more than US$1 million living in Tokyo in 2024, the third highest number in the world.^[\[164\]](#cite_note-166)^{#cite_ref-166}  

### Finance {#Finance}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: Finance")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Bank_of_Japan_2010.jpg/260px-Bank_of_Japan_2010.jpg)](/wiki/File:Bank_of_Japan_2010.jpg) [Bank of Japan](/wiki/Bank_of_Japan "Bank of Japan"), the central bank of the country, [Chuo, Tokyo](/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D,_Tokyo "Chūō, Tokyo")

Tokyo is a major international finance center.^[\[165\]](#cite_note-167)^ It houses the headquarters of several of the world's largest [investment banks](/wiki/Investment_bank "Investment bank") and insurance companies and serves as a hub for Japan's transportation, publishing, [electronics](/wiki/Electronics "Electronics") and broadcasting industries. During the centralized growth of Japan's economy following [World War II](/wiki/World_War_II "World War II"), many large firms moved their headquarters from cities such as [Osaka](/wiki/Osaka "Osaka") (the historical commercial capital) to Tokyo, in an attempt to take advantage of better access to the government.{#cite_ref-167}
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/The_Tokyo_Stock_Exchange_-_main_room_2.jpg/260px-The_Tokyo_Stock_Exchange_-_main_room_2.jpg)](/wiki/File:The_Tokyo_Stock_Exchange_-_main_room_2.jpg) The [Tokyo Stock Exchange](/wiki/Tokyo_Stock_Exchange "Tokyo Stock Exchange"), [Chuo, Tokyo](/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D,_Tokyo "Chūō, Tokyo")

Tokyo emerged as a leading international [financial center](/wiki/Financial_centre "Financial centre") (IFC) in the 1960s and has been described as one of the three "command centers" for the [world economy](/wiki/World_economy "World economy"), along with New York City and [London](/wiki/London "London").^[\[166\]](#cite_note-168)^ In the 2020 [Global Financial Centers Index](/wiki/Global_Financial_Centres_Index "Global Financial Centres Index"), Tokyo was ranked as having the fourth most competitive financial center in the world, and second most competitive in Asia (after Shanghai).^[\[167\]](#cite_note-GFCI2-169)^ [Mitsubishi UFJ](/wiki/Mitsubishi_UFJ_Financial_Group "Mitsubishi UFJ Financial Group"), [Sumitomo-Mitsui Banking Corporation](/wiki/Sumitomo_Mitsui_Banking_Corporation "Sumitomo Mitsui Banking Corporation"), [Mizuho Financial Group](/wiki/Mizuho_Financial_Group "Mizuho Financial Group"), all among the [top 20 banks in the world by total assets in 2023](/wiki/List_of_largest_banks "List of largest banks"), are headquartered in Tokyo.{#cite_ref-GFCI2_169-0}

The Japanese financial market opened up slowly in 1984 and accelerated its internationalization with the "Japanese Big Bang" in 1998.^[\[168\]](#cite_note-170)^ Despite the emergence of Singapore and Hong Kong as competing financial centers, the Tokyo IFC manages to keep a prominent position in Asia. The [Tokyo Stock Exchange](/wiki/Tokyo_Stock_Exchange "Tokyo Stock Exchange") is Japan's largest [stock exchange](/wiki/Stock_exchange "Stock exchange"), and third largest in the world by [market capitalization](/wiki/Market_capitalization "Market capitalization"), and the fourth largest by share turnover. In 1990 at the end of the [Japanese asset price bubble](/wiki/Japanese_asset_price_bubble "Japanese asset price bubble"), it accounted for more than 60% of the world stock market value.^[\[169\]](#cite_note-171)^{#cite_ref-171}  

### Media and communications {#Media_and_communications}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: Media and communications")\]  
See also: [Mass media in Japan](/wiki/Mass_media_in_Japan "Mass media in Japan")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Otemachi_1st_Square.jpg/170px-Otemachi_1st_Square.jpg)](/wiki/File:Otemachi_1st_Square.jpg)  
Otemachi 1st Square, headquarters of [NTT](/wiki/Nippon_Telegraph_and_Telephone "Nippon Telegraph and Telephone")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/FCG_7F.jpg/150px-FCG_7F.jpg)](/wiki/File:FCG_7F.jpg)  
[Fuji Broadcasting Center](/wiki/Fuji_Broadcasting_Center "Fuji Broadcasting Center"), headquarters of [Fuji Television](/wiki/Fuji_Television "Fuji Television")

Tokyo's position as the country's cultural, political, and economic hub has made its media industry the largest in Japan. A majority of national media companies are headquartered in Tokyo, as well as the Asian or Japanese branches of international media companies. The [NHK](/wiki/NHK "NHK"), the oldest and only nationwide public broadcaster in the country, is headquartered in [Shibuya](/wiki/Shibuya "Shibuya"). Other national broadcasters,^[\[170\]](#cite_note-:5-172)^ such as [TBS](/wiki/TBS_Television_(Japan) "TBS Television (Japan)"), [Nippon Television](/wiki/Nippon_Television "Nippon Television"), [Fuji Television](/wiki/Fuji_Television "Fuji Television"), and [TV Asahi](/wiki/TV_Asahi "TV Asahi"), are also based in Tokyo. Of the [five major national newspapers](/wiki/Japanese_newspapers "Japanese newspapers"),^[\[170\]](#cite_note-:5-172)^ *[The Nikkei](/wiki/The_Nikkei "The Nikkei")* , [*The Mainichi*](/wiki/Mainichi_Shimbun "Mainichi Shimbun"), and [*The Yomiuri*](/wiki/Yomiuri_Shimbun "Yomiuri Shimbun") are headquartered in Tokyo, while the other two, [*The Asahi*](/wiki/The_Asahi_Shimbun "The Asahi Shimbun") and [*The Sankei*](/wiki/Sankei_Shimbun "Sankei Shimbun"), maintain head offices both in Tokyo and [Osaka](/wiki/Osaka "Osaka"). Major publishers based in Tokyo include [Shueisha](/wiki/Shueisha "Shueisha"), [Kodansha](/wiki/Kodansha "Kodansha"), [Kadokawa](/wiki/Kadokawa_Corporation "Kadokawa Corporation"), [Shogakukan](/wiki/Shogakukan "Shogakukan"), [Bungeishunju](/wiki/Bungeishunj%C5%AB "Bungeishunjū"), [Shinchosha](/wiki/Shinchosha "Shinchosha"), and [Iwanami Shoten](/wiki/Iwanami_Shoten "Iwanami Shoten"), with a high concentration in [Chiyoda](/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo "Chiyoda, Tokyo") and [Shinjuku](/wiki/Shinjuku "Shinjuku").{#cite_ref-:5_172-1}

[Dentsu](/wiki/Dentsu "Dentsu"), [Hakuhodo](/wiki/Hakuhodo "Hakuhodo"), and [ADK Holdings](/wiki/Asatsu-DK "Asatsu-DK"), all based in Tokyo, are the country's largest advertising agencies. All three major telecommunications companies in Japan, namely [NTT](/wiki/Nippon_Telegraph_and_Telephone "Nippon Telegraph and Telephone") (whose market capitalization was once the largest among all publicly traded companies in the world),^[\[71\]](#cite_note-:4-73)^ [KDDI](/wiki/KDDI "KDDI"), and [SoftBank](/wiki/SoftBank_Group "SoftBank Group"), are based in Tokyo. Tokyo is also a major hub for anime production, with major anime studios such as [Studio Ghibli](/wiki/Studio_Ghibli "Studio Ghibli"), [Gainax](/wiki/Gainax "Gainax"), [Madhouse](/wiki/Madhouse,_Inc. "Madhouse, Inc."), [A-1 Pictures](/wiki/A-1_Pictures "A-1 Pictures"), [MAPPA](/wiki/MAPPA "MAPPA"), [Wit Studio](/wiki/Wit_Studio "Wit Studio"), [Toei](/wiki/Toei_Animation "Toei Animation"), and [Shaft](/wiki/Shaft_(company) "Shaft (company)") based particularly in the west of the metropolis.{#cite_ref-:4_73-1}  

### Tourism {#Tourism}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: Tourism")\]  
Main article: [Tourism in Tokyo](/wiki/Tourism_in_Tokyo "Tourism in Tokyo")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Hozomon_with_visitors_under_their_umbrellas%2C_a_rainy_day_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/251px-Hozomon_with_visitors_under_their_umbrellas%2C_a_rainy_day_in_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hozomon_with_visitors_under_their_umbrellas,_a_rainy_day_in_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg) [Sensoji](/wiki/Sens%C5%8D-ji "Sensō-ji") in [Asakusa](/wiki/Asakusa "Asakusa"), a popular tourist attraction

In 2019, tourism accounted for slightly more than one percent of Tokyo's total economic output, with 15.18 million foreign visitors spending 1.26 trillion yen, according to the Tokyo Metropolitan Government.^[\[171\]](#cite_note-173)^{#cite_ref-173}

According to a 2022 government survey, the most visited areas in Tokyo were:^[\[172\]](#cite_note-174)^{#cite_ref-174}

* [Shibuya](/wiki/Shibuya "Shibuya"): [Shibuya Crossing](/wiki/Shibuya_Crossing "Shibuya Crossing"), [Yoyogi Park](/wiki/Yoyogi_Park "Yoyogi Park"), [Hachiko statue](/wiki/Hachik%C5%8D "Hachikō")
* [Shinjuku](/wiki/Shinjuku "Shinjuku"): [Kabukicho](/wiki/Kabukich%C5%8D "Kabukichō"), [Shinjuku Gyoen](/wiki/Shinjuku_Gyo-en "Shinjuku Gyo-en")
* [Ginza](/wiki/Ginza "Ginza"): Shopping district, [Kabukiza](/wiki/Kabuki-za "Kabuki-za")
* [Marunouchi](/wiki/Marunouchi "Marunouchi")/[Nihombashi](/wiki/Nihonbashi "Nihonbashi"): [Imperial Palace](/wiki/Tokyo_Imperial_Palace "Tokyo Imperial Palace"), Shopping district ([Mitsukoshi](/wiki/Mitsukoshi "Mitsukoshi"), [Takashimaya](/wiki/Takashimaya "Takashimaya"))
* [Asakusa](/wiki/Asakusa "Asakusa"): [Sensoji](/wiki/Sens%C5%8D-ji "Sensō-ji"), [Tokyo Skytree](/wiki/Tokyo_Skytree "Tokyo Skytree")
* [Akihabara](/wiki/Akihabara "Akihabara"): electronics and anime culture
* [Ueno](/wiki/Ueno "Ueno"): [National Museum](/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum "Tokyo National Museum"), [National Science Museum](/wiki/National_science_museum_of_japan "National science museum of japan"), [Ueno zoo](/wiki/Ueno_Zoo "Ueno Zoo"), [Ueno Park](/wiki/Ueno_Park "Ueno Park")
* [Harajuku](/wiki/Harajuku "Harajuku")/[Omoesando](/wiki/Omotesand%C5%8D "Omotesandō"): [Meiji Shrine](/wiki/Meiji_Shrine "Meiji Shrine"), [Takeshita dori](/wiki/Takeshita_dori "Takeshita dori")
* [Roppongi](/wiki/Roppongi "Roppongi"): [Roppongi Hills](/wiki/Roppongi_Hills "Roppongi Hills"), [Azabudai Hills](/wiki/Azabudai_Hills "Azabudai Hills"), [National Arts Center Tokyo](/wiki/The_National_Art_Center,_Tokyo "The National Art Center, Tokyo"), [Suntory Art Museum](/wiki/Suntory_Museum_of_Art "Suntory Museum of Art"), [Mori Art Museum](/wiki/Mori_Art_Museum "Mori Art Museum")
* [Ikebukuro](/wiki/Ikebukuro "Ikebukuro"): Shopping district

Luxury hotels in Tokyo include the [Imperial Hotel](/wiki/Imperial_Hotel,_Tokyo "Imperial Hotel, Tokyo") (opened in 1890), [Hotel Chinzanso Tokyo](/wiki/Hotel_Chinzanso_Tokyo "Hotel Chinzanso Tokyo") (opened in 1992), [Hotel Okura Tokyo](/wiki/Hotel_Okura_Tokyo "Hotel Okura Tokyo") (opened in 1962), [Meguro Gajoen Hotel](/wiki/Meguro_Gajoen "Meguro Gajoen"), Conrad Tokyo, the [Ritz-Carlton Tokyo](/wiki/Ritz-Carlton_Tokyo "Ritz-Carlton Tokyo") and Aman Tokyo.^[\[173\]](#cite_note-175)^{#cite_ref-175}  

### Agriculture, fishery and forestry {#Agriculture.2C_fishery_and_forestry}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=32 "Edit section: Agriculture, fishery and forestry")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Toyosu_Market_Tokyo_2.jpg/285px-Toyosu_Market_Tokyo_2.jpg)](/wiki/File:Toyosu_Market_Tokyo_2.jpg) [Toyosu Market](/wiki/Toyosu_Market "Toyosu Market"), [Koto](/wiki/K%C5%8Dt%C5%8D "Kōtō")

The [Toyosu Market](/wiki/Toyosu_Market "Toyosu Market") in Tokyo is the largest wholesale fish and seafood market in the world since it opened on October 11, 2018.^[\[174\]](#cite_note-176)^ It is also one of the largest wholesale food markets of any kind. It is located in the [Toyosu](/wiki/Toyosu "Toyosu") area of [Kōtō](/wiki/K%C5%8Dt%C5%8D "Kōtō") ward. The Toyosu Market holds strong to the traditions of its predecessor, the [Tsukiji Fish Market](/wiki/Tsukiji_Fish_Market "Tsukiji Fish Market") and [Nihonbashi](/wiki/Nihonbashi "Nihonbashi") fish market, and serves some 50,000 buyers and sellers every day. Retailers, whole-sellers, auctioneers, and public citizens alike frequent the market, creating a unique microcosm of organized chaos that continues to fuel the city and its food supply after over four centuries.^[\[175\]](#cite_note-177)^ Tokyo had 8,460 hectares (20,900 acres) of agricultural land as of 2003,^[\[176\]](#cite_note-178)^ according to the [Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Fisheries](/wiki/Ministry_of_Agriculture,_Forestry_and_Fisheries_(Japan) "Ministry of Agriculture, Forestry and Fisheries (Japan)"), placing it last among the nation's prefectures. The farmland is concentrated in Western Tokyo. Perishables such as vegetables, fruits, and flowers can be conveniently shipped to the markets in the eastern part of the prefecture.{#cite_ref-178}

With 36% of its area covered by forest, Tokyo has extensive growths of [cryptomeria](/wiki/Cryptomeria "Cryptomeria") and [Japanese cypress](/wiki/Chamaecyparis_obtusa "Chamaecyparis obtusa"), especially in the mountainous western communities of Akiruno, Ōme, Okutama, Hachiōji, Hinode, and Hinohara. Decreases in the price of timber increases in the cost of production, and advancing old age among the forestry population have resulted in a decline in Tokyo's output. In addition, pollen, especially from cryptomeria, is a major [allergen](/wiki/Seasonal_allergies "Seasonal allergies") for the nearby population centers. Tokyo Bay was once a major source of fish. Most of Tokyo's fish production comes from the outer islands, such as Izu Ōshima and Hachijō-Jima. [Skipjack tuna](/wiki/Skipjack_tuna "Skipjack tuna"), [nori](/wiki/Nori "Nori"), and *[aji](/wiki/Carangidae "Carangidae")* are among the ocean products.^[\[177\]](#cite_note-179)^{#cite_ref-179}  

Transportation {#Transportation}
--------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Transportation")\]  
Main article: [Transport in Greater Tokyo](/wiki/Transport_in_Greater_Tokyo "Transport in Greater Tokyo")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Shibuya_Crossing%2C_Aerial.jpg/230px-Shibuya_Crossing%2C_Aerial.jpg)](/wiki/File:Shibuya_Crossing,_Aerial.jpg) [Shibuya Crossing](/wiki/Shibuya_Crossing "Shibuya Crossing") symbolizes the hustle and bustle of Tokyo.

Tokyo, which is the center of the [Greater Tokyo Area](/wiki/Greater_Tokyo_Area "Greater Tokyo Area"), is Japan's largest domestic and international hub for rail and ground transportation. Public transportation within Tokyo is dominated by an extensive network of "clean and efficient"^[\[178\]](#cite_note-180)^ trains and subways run by a variety of operators, with buses, monorails and trams playing a secondary feeder role. There are up to 62 electric train lines and more than 900 train stations in Tokyo.^[\[179\]](#cite_note-181)^ [Shibuya Crossing](/wiki/Shibuya_Crossing "Shibuya Crossing") is the "world's busiest pedestrian crossing", with around 3,000 people crossing at a time.^[\[180\]](#cite_note-asahi_2016-04-22-182)^^[\[181\]](#cite_note-cnn_2019-08-25-183)^^[\[182\]](#cite_note-184)^{#cite_ref-184}  

### Rail {#Rail}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=34 "Edit section: Rail")\]  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/JR%E6%9D%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E5%9C%A8%E6%9D%A5%E7%B7%9A.jpg/218px-JR%E6%9D%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E5%9C%A8%E6%9D%A5%E7%B7%9A.jpg)](/wiki/File:JR%E6%9D%B1%E6%97%A5%E6%9C%AC%E5%9C%A8%E6%9D%A5%E7%B7%9A.jpg)  
[JR East](/wiki/East_Japan_Railway_Company "East Japan Railway Company") operates the largest commuter train network in Tokyo as well as intercity services.  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/JR_Series-N700S-J30_Nozomi-4.jpg/218px-JR_Series-N700S-J30_Nozomi-4.jpg)](/wiki/File:JR_Series-N700S-J30_Nozomi-4.jpg)  
The [Shinkansen](/wiki/Shinkansen "Shinkansen") connects major cities around the country to Tokyo.

Rail is the primary mode of transportation in Tokyo,^[\[183\]](#cite_note-185)^ which has the most extensive urban railway network in the world and an equally extensive network of surface lines. [JR East](/wiki/East_Japan_Railway_Company "East Japan Railway Company") operates Tokyo's largest railway network, including the [Yamanote Line](/wiki/Yamanote_Line "Yamanote Line") loop that circles central Tokyo. It operates rail lines throughout the entire metropolitan area of Tokyo and the rest of northeastern Honshu. JR East is also responsible for the [Shinkansen](/wiki/Shinkansen "Shinkansen") high-speed rail lines that link Tokyo and the Northeastern cities of Japan ([Joetsu Shinkansen](/wiki/J%C5%8Detsu_Shinkansen "Jōetsu Shinkansen"), [Tohoku/Hokkaido Shinkansen](/wiki/T%C5%8Dhoku_Shinkansen "Tōhoku Shinkansen"), [Yamagata Shinkansen](/wiki/Yamagata_Shinkansen "Yamagata Shinkansen"), [Akita Shinkansen](/wiki/Akita_Shinkansen "Akita Shinkansen"), [Hokuriku Shinkansen](/wiki/Hokuriku_Shinkansen "Hokuriku Shinkansen")).^[\[184\]](#cite_note-186)^ The [Tokaido Shinkansen](/wiki/Tokaido_Shinkansen "Tokaido Shinkansen"), which links Tokyo and [Osaka](/wiki/Osaka "Osaka") via [Nagoya](/wiki/Nagoya "Nagoya") and [Kyoto](/wiki/Kyoto "Kyoto"), as well as western cities beyond, is operated by [JR Central](/wiki/Central_Japan_Railway_Company "Central Japan Railway Company"). The [Chuo Shinkansen](/wiki/Ch%C5%AB%C5%8D_Shinkansen "Chūō Shinkansen"), the first-ever long-distance high-speed floating maglev line currently under construction, will also be operated by JR Central. Both JR companies were created from the privatization of [Japan National Railways](/wiki/Japanese_National_Railways "Japanese National Railways") in 1987. [JR Freight](/wiki/Japan_Freight_Railway_Company "Japan Freight Railway Company") does not own any part of the railway network but operates freight trains on the [JR network](/wiki/Japan_Railways_Group "Japan Railways Group"). Two different entities operate Tokyo's underground railway network: the privatized [Tokyo Metro](/wiki/Tokyo_Metro "Tokyo Metro"), which operates Tokyo Metro lines, and the governmental [Tokyo Metropolitan Bureau of Transportation](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Bureau_of_Transportation "Tokyo Metropolitan Bureau of Transportation"), which operates Toei lines. Tokyo Metro is entirely owned by the [Japanese Government](/wiki/Government_of_Japan "Government of Japan") and the [Tokyo Metropolitan Government](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Government "Tokyo Metropolitan Government") since it was privatized in 2004 (it was previously a public entity called the *Imperial Capital Rapid Transit Authority* from 1941 to 2004), but it is scheduled to go public in 2024.^[\[185\]](#cite_note-187)^ Other major railway operators in Tokyo include [Odakyu](/wiki/Odakyu_Electric_Railway "Odakyu Electric Railway"), [Tokyu](/wiki/Tokyu_Corporation "Tokyu Corporation"), [Keio](/wiki/Keio_Corporation "Keio Corporation"), [Seibu](/wiki/Seibu_Railway "Seibu Railway"), [Tobu](/wiki/Tobu_Railway "Tobu Railway"), and [Keisei](/wiki/Keisei_Electric_Railway "Keisei Electric Railway"). Although each operator directly owns its railway lines, services that travel across different lines owned by different operators are common.{#cite_ref-187}

Tokyo once had an extensive tram network, with a total distance of 213 km (*[Tokyo Toden](/wiki/Tokyo_Toden "Tokyo Toden")* ). However, similar to other major cities worldwide, the age of motorization since the 1950s made it considered unfit to share busy roads with cars. Today, only one line, the [Arakawa line](/wiki/Toden_Arakawa_Line "Toden Arakawa Line"), remains.^[\[186\]](#cite_note-188)^{#cite_ref-188}  

### Roads {#Roads}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Roads")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Shuto_expressway_harumi_jct.jpg/250px-Shuto_expressway_harumi_jct.jpg)](/wiki/File:Shuto_expressway_harumi_jct.jpg) [Shuto Expressway](/wiki/Shuto_Expressway "Shuto Expressway") near [Harumi](/wiki/Harumi,_Tokyo "Harumi, Tokyo")

Tokyo has the lowest car ownership rate among all prefectures in Japan, with 0.416 cars per household compared to the national average of 1.025 per household. This is despite Tokyo being one of the most affluent areas in the country, with a nominal GDP per capita of around US$75,000.^[\[187\]](#cite_note-189)^ A 2021 survey found that 81% of respondents without a car were satisfied with public transport and saw no need to own one.^[\[188\]](#cite_note-190)^{#cite_ref-190}

Each road in Tokyo falls into one of the following categories depending on the type of ownership: private roads, municipal roads, metropolitan roads, and expressways. As of April 1, 2022, the total length of roads in Tokyo is approximately 24,741 km (including 2,370 km of metropolitan roads), with a total area of approximately 190.31 km^2^ (including 46.30 km^2^ of metropolitan roads).^[\[189\]](#cite_note-191)^ Intercity expressways in and around Tokyo are managed by [NEXCO East](/wiki/East_Nippon_Expressway_Company "East Nippon Expressway Company"), while expressways that serve only within the Greater Tokyo Area ([Shuto Expressway](/wiki/Shuto_Expressway "Shuto Expressway")) are operated by the Metropolitan Expressway Company. Tolls are collected based on the distance travelled. The total length of the Shuto Expressway is 337.8 km, with speed limits usually set at 80 km/h or 60 km/h to reduce noise pollution and accommodate the relatively winding road shapes.^[\[190\]](#cite_note-192)^{#cite_ref-192}  

### Aviation {#Aviation}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Aviation")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Tokyo-International-Airport_Satellite.jpg/250px-Tokyo-International-Airport_Satellite.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tokyo-International-Airport_Satellite.jpg) Aerial view of [Haneda Airport](/wiki/Haneda_Airport "Haneda Airport")

The mainland portion of Tokyo is served by two international airports: [Haneda Airport](/wiki/Haneda_Airport "Haneda Airport") in [Ōta](/wiki/%C5%8Cta,_Tokyo "Ōta, Tokyo") and [Narita International Airport](/wiki/Narita_International_Airport "Narita International Airport") in neighboring [Chiba Prefecture](/wiki/Chiba_Prefecture "Chiba Prefecture"). Haneda has served as the primary airport for Tokyo since 1931. However, the Jet Age saw an exponential increase in flights, prompting the government to build a second airport. Narita was chosen as the site for this second airport in 1966, but local farmers and left-wing activists who sympathized with them protested vehemently for more than a decade (the [Sanrizuka Struggle](/wiki/Sanrizuka_Struggle "Sanrizuka Struggle")), delaying the new airport's opening until 1978. Almost all international flights were transferred to Narita Airport upon its completion, and Haneda became primarily a domestic airport.^[\[191\]](#cite_note-193)^{#cite_ref-193}

The situation changed when it was decided to expand Haneda Airport and build new runways in 2001. The new runway, Runway D, was constructed partly as a pier-like structure rather than a landfilled structure to avoid obstructing the flow of water in the bay.^[\[192\]](#cite_note-194)^ Its opening in 2010 marked the return of international flights to Haneda, which is much closer to central Tokyo. In 2023, Haneda handled 17.9 million international passengers and 60.8 million domestic passengers,^[\[193\]](#cite_note-195)^ while Narita was used by 25.4 million international passengers and 7.7 million domestic passengers.^[\[194\]](#cite_note-196)^ According to a 2023 survey, Haneda is the fifth [busiest airport in the world by passenger traffic](/wiki/List_of_busiest_airports_by_passenger_traffic "List of busiest airports by passenger traffic").{#cite_ref-196}

[Hachijō-jima](/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima "Hachijō-jima") ([Hachijojima Airport](/wiki/Hachijojima_Airport "Hachijojima Airport")), [Kōzu-shima](/wiki/K%C5%8Dzu-shima "Kōzu-shima") ([Kōzushima Airport](/wiki/K%C5%8Dzushima_Airport "Kōzushima Airport")), [Miyakejima](/wiki/Miyakejima "Miyakejima") ([Miyakejima Airport](/wiki/Miyakejima_Airport "Miyakejima Airport")), [Nii-jima](/wiki/Nii-jima "Nii-jima") ([Niijima Airport](/wiki/Niijima_Airport "Niijima Airport")), and [Izu Ōshima](/wiki/Izu_%C5%8Cshima "Izu Ōshima") ([Oshima Airport](/wiki/Oshima_Airport "Oshima Airport")), located on the [Izu Islands](/wiki/Izu_Islands "Izu Islands"), which are governed by the Tokyo Metropolis have services to Haneda and the [Chōfu Airport](/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dfu_Airport "Chōfu Airport") located in [Chōfu](/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dfu "Chōfu").  

### Water transport {#Water_transport}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Water transport")\]  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MOL_Majesty_%28ship%2C_2009%29_001.jpg/203px-MOL_Majesty_%28ship%2C_2009%29_001.jpg)](/wiki/File:MOL_Majesty_(ship,_2009)_001.jpg)  
A [Mitsui O.S.K. Lines](/wiki/Mitsui_O.S.K._Lines "Mitsui O.S.K. Lines") container ship at the [Port of Tokyo](/wiki/Port_of_Tokyo "Port of Tokyo")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Tokaikisen-niji.jpg/181px-Tokaikisen-niji.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tokaikisen-niji.jpg)  
A [Jetfoil](/wiki/Boeing_929_Jetfoil "Boeing 929 Jetfoil") operated by the [Tōkai Kisen](/wiki/T%C5%8Dkai_Kisen "Tōkai Kisen"), which serves between Tokyo and the [Izu Islands](/wiki/Izu_Islands "Izu Islands")

Water transport is the primary means of importing and exporting goods as well as connecting the [Tokyo Islands](/wiki/Tokyo_Islands "Tokyo Islands") to the mainland. According to [Lloyd's List](/wiki/Lloyd%27s_List "Lloyd's List"), the [Port of Tokyo](/wiki/Port_of_Tokyo "Port of Tokyo") handled 4,430,000 TEU of containers in 2022, making it the 46th largest port in the world.^[\[195\]](#cite_note-197)^ The Greater Tokyo Area is served by other major ports such as the [Port of Yokohama](/wiki/Port_of_Yokohama "Port of Yokohama") and the [Port of Chiba](/wiki/Port_of_Chiba "Port of Chiba") as well. Takeshiba Pier (竹芝埠頭) in [Minato](/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo "Minato, Tokyo") is used by [Tōkai Kisen](/wiki/T%C5%8Dkai_Kisen "Tōkai Kisen"), which serves islands such as [Izu Ōshima](/wiki/Izu_%C5%8Cshima "Izu Ōshima"), [Miyakejima](/wiki/Miyake-jima "Miyake-jima"), [Hachijojima](/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima "Hachijō-jima"), [Kozushima](/wiki/K%C5%8Dzu-shima "Kōzu-shima"),^[\[196\]](#cite_note-198)^ and Ogasawara Kaiun, which serves the [Ogasawara Islands](/wiki/Bonin_Islands "Bonin Islands").^[\[197\]](#cite_note-199)^ Many of these islands are accessible only by ocean routes and helicopters, as they are too small or undulating for a landing ground, making these ships the primary means of inter-island transport. There are ferry routes that connect landmarks within the mainland portion of Tokyo as well; the [Tokyo Cruise Ship](/wiki/Tokyo_Cruise_Ship "Tokyo Cruise Ship") and the [Tokyo Mizube Line](/wiki/Tokyo_Mizube_Line "Tokyo Mizube Line") operate several routes between tourist attractions such as [Asakusa](/wiki/Asakusa "Asakusa"), [Hamarikyu](/wiki/Hama-riky%C5%AB_Gardens "Hama-rikyū Gardens"), [Odaiba](/wiki/Odaiba "Odaiba"), and Shinagawa Aquarium.^[\[198\]](#cite_note-200)^ The Symphony Cruise operates two large restaurant ships that can also be hired as party venues.^[\[199\]](#cite_note-201)^{#cite_ref-201}  

Education {#Education}
----------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=38 "Edit section: Education")\]  
Main article: [Education in Tokyo](/wiki/Education_in_Tokyo "Education in Tokyo")

Tokyo is the educational, academic, and cultural hub of Japan. From primary to tertiary levels, numerous educational institutions operate in the city to cater to a diverse range of pupils and students.  

### Tertiary education {#Tertiary_education}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=39 "Edit section: Tertiary education")\]  
See also: [List of universities in Tokyo](/wiki/List_of_universities_in_Tokyo "List of universities in Tokyo")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Yasuda_Auditorium_-_Tokyo_University_3.jpg/259px-Yasuda_Auditorium_-_Tokyo_University_3.jpg)](/wiki/File:Yasuda_Auditorium_-_Tokyo_University_3.jpg) [Yasuda Auditorium](/wiki/Yasuda_Auditorium "Yasuda Auditorium"), [University of Tokyo](/wiki/University_of_Tokyo "University of Tokyo"), [Bunkyō](/wiki/Bunky%C5%8D "Bunkyō")

Tokyo is the heartland of tertiary education in the country, home to 143 authorized universities in 2020.^[\[200\]](#cite_note-202)^ This number includes the nation's most prestigious and selective universities, such as, the [University of Tokyo](/wiki/University_of_Tokyo "University of Tokyo") (*[QS](/wiki/QS_World_University_Rankings "QS World University Rankings") National* :1st), [Tokyo Institute of Technology](/wiki/Tokyo_Institute_of_Technology "Tokyo Institute of Technology") (4th), [Hitotsubashi University](/wiki/Hitotsubashi_University "Hitotsubashi University") (15th), [Waseda University](/wiki/Waseda_University "Waseda University") (9th), and [Keio University](/wiki/Keio_University "Keio University") (10th).^[\[201\]](#cite_note-203)^ Additionally, [Tokyo University of the Arts](/wiki/Tokyo_University_of_the_Arts "Tokyo University of the Arts") is widely regarded as the most prestigious painting, sculpture, crafts, and music school in the country.^[\[202\]](#cite_note-204)^ The [United Nations University](/wiki/United_Nations_University "United Nations University"), which is the academic arm of the [United Nations](/wiki/United_Nations "United Nations"), is headquartered in [Shibuya](/wiki/Shibuya "Shibuya"), Tokyo. In 2024, *QS Best Student Cities* ranked Tokyo as the second-best city for university students, after [London](/wiki/London "London").^[\[203\]](#cite_note-205)^ The ranking noted that Tokyo is ideal for 'those who favour total immersion in the local culture rather than living in a "student bubble"', stating that despite having high-ranking universities and large global companies offering internships as well as rich culture, Tokyo still has a very low international student population ratio.^[\[204\]](#cite_note-206)^{#cite_ref-206}  

### Primary and secondary education {#Primary_and_secondary_education}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=40 "Edit section: Primary and secondary education")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Hibiya-Highschool-00.jpg/259px-Hibiya-Highschool-00.jpg)](/wiki/File:Hibiya-Highschool-00.jpg) [Hibiya High School](/wiki/Hibiya_High_School "Hibiya High School"), [Chiyoda](/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo "Chiyoda, Tokyo")

At the secondary level, 429 senior high schools are located in Tokyo, six of which are national, 186 are public, and 237 are private.^[\[205\]](#cite_note-:0-207)^ Some senior high schools, often prestigious national or private ones, run jointly with their affiliated junior high schools, providing six-year educational programs (*Chūkō Ikkan Kyōiku* ). The [Kaisei Academy](/wiki/Kaisei_Academy "Kaisei Academy"),^[\[206\]](#cite_note-208)^ [Komaba Junior \& Senior High School, University of Tsukuba](/wiki/Komaba_Junior_and_Senior_High_School,_University_of_Tsukuba "Komaba Junior and Senior High School, University of Tsukuba"),^[\[207\]](#cite_note-209)^ [Azabu High School](/wiki/Azabu_High_School "Azabu High School"), and [Oin Junior and Senior High School](/wiki/Oin_Junior_and_Senior_High_School "Oin Junior and Senior High School"),^[\[208\]](#cite_note-210)^ the largest sources of successful applicants to the nation's top university, the University of Tokyo,^[\[209\]](#cite_note-211)^ are some examples of such. At the primary level, there are 1332 elementary schools in Tokyo. Six of them are national, 1261 are public, and 53 are private.^[\[205\]](#cite_note-:0-207)^{#cite_ref-:0_207-1}

Early-modern-established academies such as [Gakushuin](/wiki/Gakush%C5%ABin "Gakushūin") and [Keio](/wiki/Keio_Gijuku_(Gakk%C5%8D_H%C5%8Djin) "Keio Gijuku (Gakkō Hōjin)") provide all-through educational programs from primary schools to universities, originally to cater to the needs of traditionally affluent and powerful families.^[\[210\]](#cite_note-212)^ There are international and ethnic schools that abide by the national curricula of their respective countries or international curricula rather than the Japanese one as well, such as the [British School in Tokyo](/wiki/The_British_School_in_Tokyo "The British School in Tokyo"), [Tokyo Chinese School](/wiki/Tokyo_Chinese_School "Tokyo Chinese School"), the [American School in Japan](/wiki/American_School_in_Japan "American School in Japan"), and the [Tokyo International School](/wiki/Tokyo_International_School "Tokyo International School").{#cite_ref-212}  

### Learned societies {#Learned_societies}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=41 "Edit section: Learned societies")\]

Almost all major Japanese learned societies are based in Tokyo. The [Japan Academy](/wiki/Japan_Academy "Japan Academy"), the country's [academy of sciences](/wiki/Academy_of_sciences "Academy of sciences"), was established in 1879 to bring together leading scholars in various disciplines.^[\[211\]](#cite_note-213)^ The [Japan Art Academy](/wiki/Japan_Art_Academy "Japan Art Academy") was established in 1919 with a similar purpose.^[\[212\]](#cite_note-214)^ These two national academies are headquartered in [Ueno Park](/wiki/Ueno_Park "Ueno Park"). The newest national academy, the [Science Council of Japan](/wiki/Science_Council_of_Japan "Science Council of Japan"), was established in 1949 to promote scientific research and the application of research findings to civilian life. It is located in [Roppongi, Minato.](/wiki/Roppongi "Roppongi"){#cite_ref-214}  

Culture {#Culture}
------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=42 "Edit section: Culture")\]  

### Museums, art galleries, libraries, and zoos {#Museums.2C_art_galleries.2C_libraries.2C_and_zoos}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=43 "Edit section: Museums, art galleries, libraries, and zoos")\]  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tokyo_National_Museum%2C_Honkan_2010.jpg/230px-Tokyo_National_Museum%2C_Honkan_2010.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_National_Museum,_Honkan_2010.jpg)  
[Tokyo National Museum](/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum "Tokyo National Museum"), [Ueno](/wiki/Ueno "Ueno")  
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Tokyo-sealife-park.JPG/204px-Tokyo-sealife-park.JPG)](/wiki/File:Tokyo-sealife-park.JPG)  
[Tokyo Sea Life Park](/wiki/Tokyo_Sea_Life_Park "Tokyo Sea Life Park"), [Edogawa](/wiki/Edogawa,_Tokyo "Edogawa, Tokyo")  
See also: [List of museums in Tokyo](/wiki/List_of_museums_in_Tokyo "List of museums in Tokyo")

Tokyo is home to a wide array of museums, art galleries, and libraries, catering to various interests. [Ueno Park](/wiki/Ueno_Park "Ueno Park") has the [Tokyo National Museum](/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum "Tokyo National Museum"), the country's largest museum specializing in traditional Japanese art,^[\[213\]](#cite_note-215)^ the [National Museum of Western Art](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Western_Art "National Museum of Western Art"), whose building designed by [Le Corbusier](/wiki/Le_Corbusier "Le Corbusier") is a [world heritage site](/wiki/World_Heritage_Site "World Heritage Site"),^[\[214\]](#cite_note-216)^ and the [National Museum of Nature and Science](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Nature_and_Science "National Museum of Nature and Science"). [Ueno Zoo](/wiki/Ueno_Zoo "Ueno Zoo") is also located within the park, near the [Shinobazu Pond](/wiki/Shinobazu_Pond "Shinobazu Pond"). It is famous for being one of the three zoos in Japan to have [giant pandas](/wiki/Giant_panda "Giant panda"), with a population of 4 as of May 2024.^[\[215\]](#cite_note-217)^ Other notable museums include the [Artizon Museum](/wiki/Artizon_Museum "Artizon Museum") in Chūō, the [National Museum of Emerging Science and Innovation](/wiki/Miraikan "Miraikan") in Odaiba, and the [Edo-Tokyo Museum](/wiki/Edo-Tokyo_Museum "Edo-Tokyo Museum") in Sumida, which provides insights into the history and culture of Tokyo. The [Edo-Tokyo Open Air Architectural Museum](/wiki/Edo-Tokyo_Open_Air_Architectural_Museum "Edo-Tokyo Open Air Architectural Museum") preserves various buildings that have existed throughout the history of Tokyo. The [Nezu Museum](/wiki/Nezu_Museum "Nezu Museum") in Aoyama has a collection of pre-modern Japanese and East Asian art. Located near the [Imperial Palace](/wiki/Tokyo_Imperial_Palace "Tokyo Imperial Palace"), the [National Diet Library](/wiki/National_Diet_Library "National Diet Library"), the [National Archives](/wiki/National_Archives_of_Japan "National Archives of Japan"), and the [National Museum of Modern Art](/wiki/National_Museum_of_Modern_Art,_Tokyo "National Museum of Modern Art, Tokyo") are also notable cultural institutions. Additionally, the [Mori Art Museum](/wiki/Mori_Art_Museum "Mori Art Museum") in [Roppongi](/wiki/Roppongi "Roppongi") and the [Sumida Hokusai Museum](/w/index.php?title=Sumida_Hokusai_Museum&action=edit&redlink=1 "Sumida Hokusai Museum (page does not exist)") in Sumida ward are notable for their contemporary and ukiyo-e art collections, respectively. The [Sompo Museum of Art](/wiki/Sompo_Museum_of_Art "Sompo Museum of Art") in Shinjuku is best known for owning one of Gogh's [*Sunflowers*](/wiki/Sunflowers_(Van_Gogh_series) "Sunflowers (Van Gogh series)"). The [Tokyo Metropolitan Garden Art Museum](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Teien_Art_Museum "Tokyo Metropolitan Teien Art Museum") in [Minato](/wiki/Minato,_Tokyo "Minato, Tokyo") features the former Tokyo House of [Yasuhiko, Prince Asaka](/wiki/Prince_Yasuhiko_Asaka "Prince Yasuhiko Asaka"), which was built in an opulent [Art Deco](/wiki/Art_Deco "Art Deco") style in 1933. The [Railway Museum](/wiki/Railway_Museum_(Saitama) "Railway Museum (Saitama)"), which used to be located in [Kanda](/wiki/Kanda,_Tokyo "Kanda, Tokyo"), has relocated to a larger site in [Omiya, Saitama](/wiki/%C5%8Cmiya,_Saitama "Ōmiya, Saitama") and stores 42 train carriages and locomotives of historical importance.^[\[216\]](#cite_note-218)^ The [Tobacco and Salt Museum](/wiki/Tobacco_and_Salt_Museum "Tobacco and Salt Museum") in Sumida has one of the world's most extensive collections of different types of tobacco and salt. Major aquariums in Tokyo include: [Shinagawa Aquarium](/w/index.php?title=Shinagawa_Aquarium&action=edit&redlink=1 "Shinagawa Aquarium (page does not exist)"), [Tokyo Sea Life Park](/wiki/Tokyo_Sea_Life_Park "Tokyo Sea Life Park"), [Sunshine Aquarium](/wiki/Sunshine_Aquarium "Sunshine Aquarium") and [Sumida Aquarium](/wiki/Sumida_Aquarium "Sumida Aquarium").{#cite_ref-218}  

### Leisure and entertainment {#Leisure_and_entertainment}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=44 "Edit section: Leisure and entertainment")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Tokyo_International_Forum%2C_Tokyo%3B_April_2021_%2858%29.jpg/260px-Tokyo_International_Forum%2C_Tokyo%3B_April_2021_%2858%29.jpg)](/wiki/File:Tokyo_International_Forum,_Tokyo;_April_2021_(58).jpg) [Tokyo International Forum](/wiki/Tokyo_International_Forum "Tokyo International Forum"), a multi-purpose cultural center in [Chiyoda](/wiki/Chiyoda,_Tokyo "Chiyoda, Tokyo") [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Kabukicho_red_gate_and_colorful_neon_street_signs_at_night%2C_Shinjuku%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/260px-Kabukicho_red_gate_and_colorful_neon_street_signs_at_night%2C_Shinjuku%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg)](/wiki/File:Kabukicho_red_gate_and_colorful_neon_street_signs_at_night,_Shinjuku,_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg) [Kabukicho](/wiki/Kabukich%C5%8D "Kabukichō"), a nightlife district in [Shinjuku](/wiki/Shinjuku "Shinjuku")

Tokyo offers a diverse array of leisure and entertainment options. The city is home to numerous theatres. The [National Noh Theatre](/wiki/National_Noh_Theatre "National Noh Theatre") and [Kabuki-za](/wiki/Kabuki-za "Kabuki-za") are dedicated to traditional Japanese plays. The [New National Theatre Tokyo](/wiki/New_National_Theatre_Tokyo "New National Theatre Tokyo") in Shibuya serves as a central venue for opera, ballet, contemporary dance, and drama.^[\[217\]](#cite_note-about-nntt-219)^ Other major play and concert venues include: the [National Theatre of Japan](/wiki/National_Theatre_of_Japan "National Theatre of Japan"), the [Imperial Theatre](/wiki/Imperial_Theatre_(Japan) "Imperial Theatre (Japan)"), the [Meiji-za](/wiki/Meiji-za "Meiji-za"), the [NHK Hall](/wiki/NHK_Hall "NHK Hall"), the [Tokyo Metropolitan Theatre](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Theatre "Tokyo Metropolitan Theatre"), [Tokyo Opera City](/wiki/Tokyo_Opera_City_Tower "Tokyo Opera City Tower") and the [Tokyo International Forum](/wiki/Tokyo_International_Forum "Tokyo International Forum"). Two sports venues, the [Nippon Budokan](/wiki/Nippon_Budokan "Nippon Budokan") and the [Tokyo Dome](/wiki/Tokyo_Dome "Tokyo Dome"), are usually used to host concerts by popular pop artists.^[\[218\]](#cite_note-220)^{#cite_ref-220}

The nightlife district of Tokyo is centered around areas in the west of the city such as [Shibuya](/wiki/Shibuya "Shibuya"), [Shinjuku](/wiki/Shinjuku "Shinjuku"), and [Roppongi](/wiki/Roppongi "Roppongi"), with high a concentration of bars, clubs, [host and hostess clubs](/wiki/Host_and_hostess_clubs "Host and hostess clubs"), and live music venues.^[\[219\]](#cite_note-221)^ Tokyo is also known for its festivals, such as the [Sannō Matsuri](/wiki/Sann%C5%8D_Matsuri "Sannō Matsuri") at [Hie Shrine](/wiki/Hie_Shrine "Hie Shrine"), the Sanja Festival at Asakusa Shrine, and the biennial [Kanda Matsuri](/wiki/Kanda_Matsuri "Kanda Matsuri"), which features parades with elaborately decorated floats. [Harajuku](/wiki/Harajuku "Harajuku"), located in Shibuya, is internationally famous for its youth fashion and street culture, with trendy shops, cafes, and Takeshita Street.^[\[220\]](#cite_note-222)^ [Akihabara](/wiki/Akihabara "Akihabara"), known as "Electric Town", is a hub for electronics and [otaku culture](/wiki/Otaku "Otaku") such as [anime](/wiki/Anime "Anime") and [computer games](/wiki/Video_game "Video game"), with numerous shops selling anime, manga, and gaming merchandise.^[\[221\]](#cite_note-223)^ [Ginza](/wiki/Ginza "Ginza") and [Nihombashi](/wiki/Nihonbashi "Nihonbashi") are two of Tokyo's most notable shopping districts. Ginza is known for its high-end shopping, featuring luxury brand stores, boutique shops, and department stores such as [Mitsukoshi](/wiki/Mitsukoshi "Mitsukoshi") and [Wako](/wiki/Wako_(retailer) "Wako (retailer)"). It is also home to numerous fine dining places and art galleries, making it a cultural and commercial hub. Nihombashi, historically a center of commerce, has long-established shops and the Mitsukoshi department flagship store, Japan's first department store, founded in 1673.^[\[222\]](#cite_note-224)^ [Jinbōchō](/wiki/Jinb%C5%8Dch%C5%8D,_Tokyo "Jinbōchō, Tokyo") is known for its concentration of bookstores, publishing houses, and literary cafes, and its links to a large number of famous literary figures.^[\[223\]](#cite_note-225)^{#cite_ref-225}

Modern attractions in Tokyo include the [Tokyo Skytree](/wiki/Tokyo_Skytree "Tokyo Skytree") in Sumida, the tallest structure in Japan, which provides panoramic views of the city from its observation decks. [Odaiba](/wiki/Odaiba "Odaiba"), a man-made island in Tokyo Bay, features shopping, dining, and entertainment attractions such as the [teamLab Planets](/wiki/TeamLab_Planets_TOKYO_DMM.com "TeamLab Planets TOKYO DMM.com") digital art museum and [Joypolis](/wiki/Joypolis "Joypolis") indoor amusement park.^[\[224\]](#cite_note-226)^ The [Tokyo Disney Resort](/wiki/Tokyo_Disney_Resort "Tokyo Disney Resort") and its two theme parks [Tokyo Disneyland](/wiki/Tokyo_Disneyland "Tokyo Disneyland") and [Tokyo DisneySea](/wiki/Tokyo_DisneySea "Tokyo DisneySea") are major destinations for family entertainment. Although these Disney theme parks bear the name Tokyo, they are located in nearby [Urayasu, Chiba](/wiki/Urayasu "Urayasu"), just east of Tokyo.{#cite_ref-226}  

### Food {#Food}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=45 "Edit section: Food")\]

In November 2007, [Michelin](/wiki/Michelin_guide "Michelin guide") released their first guide for fine dining in Tokyo, awarding 191 stars in total, or about twice as many as Tokyo's nearest competitor, Paris. As of 2017, 227 restaurants in Tokyo have been awarded (92 in Paris). Twelve establishments were awarded the maximum of three stars (Paris has 10), 54 received two stars, and 161 earned one star.^[\[225\]](#cite_note-227)^{#cite_ref-227}  

### Recreation {#Recreation}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=46 "Edit section: Recreation")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg/220px-Ogasawara_Islands%2C_Tokyo%2C_Japan.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ogasawara_Islands,_Tokyo,_Japan.jpg) [Ogasawara National Park](/wiki/Ogasawara_National_Park "Ogasawara National Park"), a [UNESCO](/wiki/UNESCO "UNESCO") World Natural Heritage Site

Natural settings for outdoor activities include [Okutama](/wiki/Okutama,_Tokyo "Okutama, Tokyo") and [Mount Takao](/wiki/Mount_Takao "Mount Takao"), which are known for their hiking trails and scenic views. [Kasai Seaside Park](/wiki/Kasai_Rinkai_Park "Kasai Rinkai Park") provides coastal leisure activities. [Ueno Park](/wiki/Ueno_Park "Ueno Park") houses several museums, and a zoo, and is famous for its cherry blossoms. [Inokashira Park](/wiki/Inokashira_Park "Inokashira Park") in Kichijoji features a pond, a zoo, and in its vicinity the [Ghibli Museum](/wiki/Ghibli_Museum "Ghibli Museum"). [Yoyogi Park](/wiki/Yoyogi_Park "Yoyogi Park"), located near Shibuya, is popular for picnics and outdoor events. [Shinjuku Gyoen National Garden](/wiki/Shinjuku_Gyo-en "Shinjuku Gyo-en"), [Koishikawa Korakuen Garden](/wiki/Koishikawa-K%C5%8Drakuen "Koishikawa-Kōrakuen"), [Rikugien Garden](/wiki/Rikugi-en_Gardens "Rikugi-en Gardens"), [Hamarikyu Gardens](/wiki/Hama-riky%C5%AB_Gardens "Hama-rikyū Gardens"), [Kiyosumi Garden](/wiki/Kiyosumi_Garden "Kiyosumi Garden"), [Kyu Shiba Rikyu Garden](/wiki/Ky%C5%AB_Shiba_Riky%C5%AB_Garden "Kyū Shiba Rikyū Garden"), [Chinzanso Garden](/wiki/Chinzan-s%C5%8D_Garden "Chinzan-sō Garden"), [Happo-en Garden](/w/index.php?title=Happo-en_Garden&action=edit&redlink=1 "Happo-en Garden (page does not exist)"),^[\[226\]](#cite_note-228)^ [Mukojima-Hyakkaen Garden](/wiki/Muk%C5%8Djima-Hyakkaen_Garden "Mukōjima-Hyakkaen Garden") and [Meiji Jingu Inner Garden](/wiki/Meiji_Shrine_Inner_Garden "Meiji Shrine Inner Garden") are popular traditional Japanese gardens in Tokyo, some of which originally belonged to members of the *[kazoku](/wiki/Kazoku "Kazoku")* nobility. Botanical gardens in Tokyo include the [University of Tokyo](/wiki/University_of_Tokyo "University of Tokyo")'s [Koishikawa Botanical Garden](/wiki/Koishikawa_Botanical_Garden "Koishikawa Botanical Garden"), the [Yumenoshima Tropical Greenhouse Dome](/wiki/Yumenoshima_Tropical_Greenhouse_Dome "Yumenoshima Tropical Greenhouse Dome"), and the [Institute for Nature Study Nature Reserve](/wiki/Institute_for_Nature_Study "Institute for Nature Study").{#cite_ref-228}  

#### National parks {#National_parks}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=47 "Edit section: National parks")\]

As of March 31, 2008, 36% of the total land area of the prefecture was designated as [Natural Parks](/wiki/Prefectural_Natural_Park "Prefectural Natural Park") (second only to [Shiga Prefecture](/wiki/Shiga_Prefecture "Shiga Prefecture")), namely the [Chichibu Tama Kai](/wiki/Chichibu_Tama_Kai_National_Park "Chichibu Tama Kai National Park"), [Fuji-Hakone-Izu](/wiki/Fuji-Hakone-Izu_National_Park "Fuji-Hakone-Izu National Park"), and [Ogasawara National Park](/wiki/Ogasawara_National_Park "Ogasawara National Park") (the last a UNESCO [World Heritage Site](/wiki/World_Heritage_Sites_in_Japan "World Heritage Sites in Japan")); [Meiji no Mori Takao Quasi-National Park](/wiki/Meiji_no_Mori_Takao_Quasi-National_Park "Meiji no Mori Takao Quasi-National Park"); and [Akikawa Kyūryō](/wiki/Akikawa_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park "Akikawa Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park"), [Hamura Kusabana Kyūryō](/wiki/Hamura_Kusabana_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park "Hamura Kusabana Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park"), [Sayama](/wiki/Sayama_Prefectural_Natural_Park_(Tokyo) "Sayama Prefectural Natural Park (Tokyo)"), [Takao Jinba](/wiki/Takao_Jinba_Prefectural_Natural_Park "Takao Jinba Prefectural Natural Park"), [Takiyama](/wiki/Takiyama_Prefectural_Natural_Park "Takiyama Prefectural Natural Park"), and [Tama Kyūryō](/wiki/Tama_Ky%C5%ABry%C5%8D_Prefectural_Natural_Park "Tama Kyūryō Prefectural Natural Park") Prefectural Natural Parks.^[\[227\]](#cite_note-229)^{#cite_ref-229}  

### In popular culture {#In_popular_culture}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=48 "Edit section: In popular culture")\]
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Illuminated_buildings_in_Akihabara%2C_west_side_of_Sotokanda_1_%282015-04-13_03.22.59_by_IQRemix%29-edited.jpg/220px-Illuminated_buildings_in_Akihabara%2C_west_side_of_Sotokanda_1_%282015-04-13_03.22.59_by_IQRemix%29-edited.jpg)](/wiki/File:Illuminated_buildings_in_Akihabara,_west_side_of_Sotokanda_1_(2015-04-13_03.22.59_by_IQRemix)-edited.jpg) [Akihabara](/wiki/Akihabara "Akihabara") is the most popular area for fans of anime, manga, and games.

As the largest population center in Japan and the site of the country's largest broadcasters and studios, Tokyo is frequently the setting for many [Japanese movies](/wiki/Cinema_of_Japan "Cinema of Japan"), television shows, animated series ([anime](/wiki/Anime "Anime")), [web comics](/wiki/Web_comics "Web comics"), [light novels](/wiki/Light_novels "Light novels"), [video games](/wiki/Video_games "Video games"), and comic books ([manga](/wiki/Manga "Manga")). In the *[kaiju](/wiki/Kaiju "Kaiju")* (monster movie) genre, landmarks of Tokyo are usually destroyed by giant monsters such as [Godzilla](/wiki/Godzilla "Godzilla") and [Gamera](/wiki/Gamera "Gamera").

Tokyo is also a popular foreign setting for non-Japanese media. Some Hollywood directors have turned to Tokyo as a backdrop for movies set in Japan. Postwar examples include *[Tokyo Joe](/wiki/Tokyo_Joe_(1949_film) "Tokyo Joe (1949 film)")* , *[My Geisha](/wiki/My_Geisha "My Geisha")* , *[Tokyo Story](/wiki/Tokyo_Story "Tokyo Story")* and the [James Bond](/wiki/James_Bond "James Bond") film *[You Only Live Twice](/wiki/You_Only_Live_Twice_(film) "You Only Live Twice (film)")* ; recent examples include *[Kill Bill](/wiki/Kill_Bill "Kill Bill")* , *[The Fast and the Furious: Tokyo Drift](/wiki/The_Fast_and_the_Furious:_Tokyo_Drift "The Fast and the Furious: Tokyo Drift")* , *[Lost in Translation](/wiki/Lost_in_Translation_(film) "Lost in Translation (film)")* , *[Babel](/wiki/Babel_(film) "Babel (film)")* , *[Inception](/wiki/Inception "Inception")* , *[The Wolverine](/wiki/The_Wolverine_(2013_film) "The Wolverine (2013 film)")* and *[Avengers: Endgame](/wiki/Avengers:_Endgame "Avengers: Endgame")*.

Japanese author [Haruki Murakami](/wiki/Haruki_Murakami "Haruki Murakami") has based some of his novels in Tokyo (including [*Norwegian Wood*](/wiki/Norwegian_Wood_(novel) "Norwegian Wood (novel)")), and [David Mitchell](/wiki/David_Mitchell_(author) "David Mitchell (author)")'s first two novels (*[number9dream](/wiki/Number9dream "Number9dream")* and [*Ghostwritten*](/wiki/Ghostwritten_(novel) "Ghostwritten (novel)")) featured the city.  

Sports {#Sports}
----------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=49 "Edit section: Sports")\]  
Main article: [Sports in Tokyo](/wiki/Sports_in_Tokyo "Sports in Tokyo")
[![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9d/New_national_stadium_tokyo_1.jpg/220px-New_national_stadium_tokyo_1.jpg)](/wiki/File:New_national_stadium_tokyo_1.jpg) [Japan National Stadium](/wiki/Japan_National_Stadium "Japan National Stadium") [![](//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Ryogoku_Kokugikan_Tsuriyane_05212006.jpg/220px-Ryogoku_Kokugikan_Tsuriyane_05212006.jpg)](/wiki/File:Ryogoku_Kokugikan_Tsuriyane_05212006.jpg) [Ryōgoku Kokugikan](/wiki/Ry%C5%8Dgoku_Kokugikan "Ryōgoku Kokugikan") sumo wrestling arena

Tokyo, with a diverse array of sports, is home to two professional baseball clubs, the [Yomiuri Giants](/wiki/Yomiuri_Giants "Yomiuri Giants") who play at the [Tokyo Dome](/wiki/Tokyo_Dome "Tokyo Dome") and [Tokyo Yakult Swallows](/wiki/Tokyo_Yakult_Swallows "Tokyo Yakult Swallows") at [Meiji-Jingu Stadium](/wiki/Meiji-Jingu_Stadium "Meiji-Jingu Stadium"). The [Japan Sumo Association](/wiki/Japan_Sumo_Association "Japan Sumo Association") is also headquartered in Tokyo at the [Ryōgoku Kokugikan](/wiki/Ry%C5%8Dgoku_Kokugikan "Ryōgoku Kokugikan") sumo arena where three official [sumo](/wiki/Sumo "Sumo") tournaments are held annually (in January, May, and September). Soccer clubs in Tokyo include [FC Tokyo](/wiki/FC_Tokyo "FC Tokyo") and [Tokyo Verdy](/wiki/Tokyo_Verdy "Tokyo Verdy"), both of which play at [Ajinomoto Stadium](/wiki/Ajinomoto_Stadium "Ajinomoto Stadium") in [Chōfu](/wiki/Ch%C5%8Dfu,_Tokyo "Chōfu, Tokyo"), and [Machida Zelvia](/wiki/FC_Machida_Zelvia "FC Machida Zelvia") at [Machida GION Stadium](/wiki/Machida_GION_Stadium "Machida GION Stadium") in [Machida](/wiki/Machida,_Tokyo "Machida, Tokyo"). [Rugby Union](/wiki/Rugby_union "Rugby union") is also played in Tokyo, with multiple [Japan Rugby League One](/wiki/Japan_Rugby_League_One "Japan Rugby League One") clubs based in the city including: [Black Rams Tokyo](/wiki/Black_Rams_Tokyo "Black Rams Tokyo") ([Setagaya](/wiki/Setagaya,_Tokyo "Setagaya, Tokyo")), [Tokyo Sungoliath](/wiki/Tokyo_Sungoliath "Tokyo Sungoliath") ([Fuchū](/wiki/Fuch%C5%AB,_Tokyo "Fuchū, Tokyo")) and [Toshiba Brave Lupus Tokyo](/wiki/Toshiba_Brave_Lupus_Tokyo "Toshiba Brave Lupus Tokyo") ([Fuchū](/wiki/Fuch%C5%AB,_Tokyo "Fuchū, Tokyo")).

Basketball clubs include the [Hitachi SunRockers](/wiki/Hitachi_SunRockers "Hitachi SunRockers"), [Toyota Alvark Tokyo](/wiki/Toyota_Alvark_Tokyo "Toyota Alvark Tokyo"), and [Tokyo Excellence](/wiki/Tokyo_Excellence "Tokyo Excellence").

Tokyo hosted the [1964 Summer Olympics](/wiki/1964_Summer_Olympics "1964 Summer Olympics"), thus becoming the first Asian city to host the [Summer Games](/wiki/Summer_Olympic_Games "Summer Olympic Games"). The National Stadium, also known as the [Olympic Stadium](/wiki/National_Stadium_(Tokyo,_1958) "National Stadium (Tokyo, 1958)"), was host to several international sporting events. In 2016, it was to be replaced by the [New National Stadium](/wiki/Japan_National_Stadium "Japan National Stadium"). With several world-class sports venues, Tokyo often hosts national and international sporting events such as basketball tournaments, women's volleyball tournaments, tennis tournaments, swim meets, marathons, rugby union and sevens rugby games, soccer exhibition games, [judo](/wiki/Judo "Judo"), and [karate](/wiki/Karate "Karate"). [Tokyo Metropolitan Gymnasium](/wiki/Tokyo_Metropolitan_Gymnasium "Tokyo Metropolitan Gymnasium"), in [Sendagaya](/wiki/Sendagaya "Sendagaya"), [Shibuya](/wiki/Shibuya "Shibuya"), is a large sports complex that includes swimming pools, training rooms, and a large indoor arena. According to *Around the Rings* , the gymnasium has played host to the October 2011 Artistic Gymnastics World Championships, despite the International Gymnastics Federation's initial doubt in Tokyo's ability to host the championships after the triple disaster hit Japan.^[\[228\]](#cite_note-230)^ Tokyo was also selected to host several games for the [2019 Rugby World Cup](/wiki/2019_Rugby_World_Cup "2019 Rugby World Cup"), and to host the [2020 Summer Olympics](/wiki/2020_Summer_Olympics "2020 Summer Olympics") and [Paralympics](/wiki/2020_Summer_Paralympics "2020 Summer Paralympics"), which had to be rescheduled to the summer of 2021 due to [COVID-19 pandemic](/wiki/COVID-19_pandemic_in_Japan "COVID-19 pandemic in Japan").{#cite_ref-230}  

International relations {#International_relations}
--------------------------------------------------

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=50 "Edit section: International relations")\]

Tokyo is the founding member of the [Asian Network of Major Cities 21](/wiki/Asian_Network_of_Major_Cities_21 "Asian Network of Major Cities 21") and is a member of the [Council of Local Authorities for International Relations](/wiki/Council_of_Local_Authorities_for_International_Relations "Council of Local Authorities for International Relations"). Tokyo was also a founding member of the [C40 Cities Climate Leadership Group](/wiki/C40_Cities_Climate_Leadership_Group "C40 Cities Climate Leadership Group").^[\[229\]](#cite_note-231)^{#cite_ref-231}  

### Sister cities and states {#Sister_cities_and_states}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=51 "Edit section: Sister cities and states")\]  
See also: [List of twin towns and sister cities in Japan](/wiki/List_of_twin_towns_and_sister_cities_in_Japan "List of twin towns and sister cities in Japan")

As of 2022^[\[update\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit)^, Tokyo has [twinning](/wiki/Twin_towns_and_sister_cities "Twin towns and sister cities") or friendship agreements with the following twelve cities and states:^[\[230\]](#cite_note-TMG-232)^{#cite_ref-TMG_232-0}  
* [New York City](/wiki/New_York_City "New York City"), United States (since February 1960)
* [Beijing](/wiki/Beijing "Beijing"), China (since March 1979)
* [Paris](/wiki/Paris "Paris"), France (since July 1982)^[\[231\]](#cite_note-P1-233)^{#cite_ref-P1_233-0}
* [New South Wales](/wiki/New_South_Wales "New South Wales"), Australia (since May 1984)
* [Seoul](/wiki/Seoul "Seoul"), South Korea (since September 1988)
* [Jakarta](/wiki/Jakarta "Jakarta"), Indonesia (since October 1989)
* [São Paulo State](/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo_(state) "São Paulo (state)"), Brazil (since June 1990)
* [Cairo](/wiki/Cairo "Cairo"), Egypt (since October 1990)
* [Moscow](/wiki/Moscow "Moscow"), Russia (since July 1991)
* [Berlin](/wiki/Berlin "Berlin"), Germany (since May 1994)
* [Rome](/wiki/Rome "Rome"), Italy (since July 1996)
* [London](/wiki/London "London"), United Kingdom (since October 2015)  

### Friendship and cooperation agreements {#Friendship_and_cooperation_agreements}

\[[edit](/w/index.php?title=Tokyo&action=edit&section=52 "Edit section: Friendship and cooperation agreements")\]

* [Tomsk Oblast](/wiki/Tomsk_Oblast "Tomsk Oblast"), Russia (since May 2015)^[\[232\]](#cite_note-234)^{#cite_ref-234}
* [Brussels](/wiki/Brussels "Brussels"), Belgium (since October 2016)^[\[233\]](#cite_note-235)^{#cite_ref-235}
* [Los Angeles County](/wiki/Los_Angeles_County,_California "Los Angeles County, California"), United States (since August 2021)^[\[234\]](#cite_note-236)^{#cite_ref-236}

Retrieved May 11, 2024. The special ward of Shinjuku is the [seat of the Tokyo Metropolitan Government](/wiki/Prefectural_capital "Prefectural capital"). Tokyo as defined in this article is a [prefecture](/wiki/Prefectures_of_Japan "Prefectures of Japan"), not a [municipality](/wiki/Municipalities_of_Japan "Municipalities of Japan"); Tokyo's special wards, cities, towns, and villages are Tokyo's municipalities. As the informal prefectural capital of Tokyo, "Tokyo" means the 23 [special wards of Tokyo](/wiki/Special_wards_of_Tokyo "Special wards of Tokyo"), i.e. former [Tokyo City](/wiki/Tokyo_City "Tokyo City"), not Tokyo, the entire prefecture/"Metropolis" as defined in this article.{#cite_note-2}
{#cite_note-2}

* Fowler, Edward. *San'ya Blues: Labouring Life in Contemporary Tokyo* . (1996) [ISBN](/wiki/ISBN_(identifier) "ISBN (identifier)") [0-8014-8570-3](/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-8014-8570-3 "Special:BookSources/0-8014-8570-3").
* Friedman, Mildred, ed. *Tokyo, Form and Spirit*. (1986). 256 pp.
* Jinnai, Hidenobu. *Tokyo: A Spatial Anthropology*. (1995). 236 pp.
* Jones, Sumie et al. eds. *A Tokyo Anthology: Literature from Japan's Modern Metropolis, 1850--1920* (2017); primary sources [excerpt](https://www.amazon.com/Tokyo-Anthology-Literature-Metropolis-1850-1920/dp/0824855906/)
* Perez, Louis G. *Tokyo: Geography, History, and Culture* (ABC-CLIO, 2019).
* Reynolds, Jonathan M. "Japan's Imperial Diet Building: Debate over Construction of a National Identity". *Art Journal*. 55#3 (1996) pp. 38+.
* Sassen, Saskia. *The Global City: New York, London, Tokyo*. (1991). 397 pp.
* Sorensen, A. *Land Readjustment and Metropolitan Growth: An Examination of Suburban Land Development and Urban Sprawl in the Tokyo Metropolitan Area* (2000)
